The barred owl has been known to consume a diversity of animals from different [taxonomic classes](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Class_(taxonomy) "Class (taxonomy)"). Primarily, these owls live off of small [mammals](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mammal "Mammal").<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-28"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Konig-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_27-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Mikkola-27">[27]</a></sup> Other [vertebrates](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertebrate "Vertebrate") are rarely neglected though, especially [birds](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bird "Bird") and [amphibians](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amphibian "Amphibian"), but also occasionally [reptiles](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reptile "Reptile") and [fish](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fish "Fish"). For an owl its size, the barred owl also consumes a large amount of [arthropods](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arthropod "Arthropod") and other [invertebrates](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Invertebrate "Invertebrate").<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-29"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Konig-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Worm_131-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Worm-131">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fcps.edu_132-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-fcps.edu-132">[132]</a></sup> One study from a wide swath of the range found that among 2234 accrued prey items, 76% were mammals, 15.8% were invertebrates, 5.8% were birds and 2.5% were other vertebrates.<sup id="cite_ref-Snyder_133-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Snyder-133">[133]</a></sup> In four other studies from different parts of the distribution, the mean balance of mammals in diet was around 64.9%, birds at around 13.4%, invertebrates at around 11.4% and different classes of vertebrates (mostly amphibians) at around 10.3%.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-15"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Takats_100-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Takats-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Elderkin_127-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Elderkin-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marks_134-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Marks-134">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bosakowski2_135-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bosakowski2-135">[135]</a></sup> A compilation study that included a total of 7077 prey items using all methodologies, 71.9% were mammals, 9.5% were birds, 0.6% reptiles, 6% amphibians, 1.89% fish, 1% [earthworms](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earthworm "Earthworm"), 0.2% [gastropods](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gastropod "Gastropod"), 6.5% [insects](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Insect "Insect") and 2.4% [crayfish](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crayfish "Crayfish").<sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup> Barred owls tend to focus on fairly small-sized prey, although are capable of attacking larger than usual prey in infrequent cases.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup> The mean size of prey taken is seldom estimated in the barred owls' eastern range, although one study estimated mean size of prey in the general east was 33.5 g (1.18 oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Marti_136-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Marti-136">[136]</a></sup> Many more studies have estimated mean prey masses in westerly areas of sympatry with spotted owls to understand how their diets may conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup> Different studies from the west (mainly [Washington](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Washington_(state) "Washington (state)") and [Oregon](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oregon "Oregon")) have variously estimated the mean prey sizes for barred owls at 47.7 g (1.68 oz), 56.1 g (1.98 oz), 60.2 g (2.12 oz), 103.5 g (3.65 oz) and 123.6 g (4.36 oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti_136-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Marti-136">[136]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hamer_138-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hamer-138">[138]</a></sup>

### Mammals [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Barred_owl&action=edit&section=10 "Edit section: Mammals")

The predominant small mammals available in forest and woodland edges are generally small [rodents](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rodent "Rodent"), so the barred owl, like other _Strix_ owls, most often relies on rodents as the primary type of food.<sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup> Preferred rodents to be taken are [voles](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vole "Vole"), [mice](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mouse "Mouse") of the genus _[Peromyscus](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peromyscus "Peromyscus")_ and assorted [rats](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rat "Rat"), including non-native _[Rattus](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rattus "Rattus")_ species as well as unrelated native types like [cotton rats](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cotton_rat "Cotton rat"), [rice rats](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oryzomys "Oryzomys") and [woodrats](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pack_rat "Pack rat"). These all share with barred owls a penchant for [nocturnality](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nocturnality "Nocturnality") and [crepuscular](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crepuscular_animal "Crepuscular animal") habits (although many voles are more correctly considered [cathemeral](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cathemerality "Cathemerality"))<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-30"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Konig-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-14"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_65-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Johnsgard-65">[65]</a></sup> While during other seasons, the diet of barred owls can be fairly diverse, the winter diet may be almost wholly rodents.<sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-5"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup> This was the case in winter in [Montana](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Montana "Montana"), where 97.6% of 1153 prey items were [montane voles](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Montane_vole "Montane vole") or [meadow voles](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meadow_vole "Meadow vole"), with a possible slight mixture of other voles.<sup id="cite_ref-Holt2_139-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Holt2-139">[139]</a></sup> The diet of barred owls in a much smaller study near [Urbana, Illinois](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Urbana,_Illinois "Urbana, Illinois") during winter was less homogeneous but still led by rodents, especially the meadow vole (32.3%) and [white-footed mouse](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/White-footed_mouse "White-footed mouse") (23.5%).<sup id="cite_ref-Cahn_140-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Cahn-140">[140]</a></sup> A winter food study in [Essex County, New Jersey](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Essex_County,_New_Jersey "Essex County, New Jersey") found that among 118 prey items, meadow voles comprised a great majority of the prey, at 91.5% of the balance.<sup id="cite_ref-Rusling_141-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Rusling-141">[141]</a></sup>

An unusual lack of diversity in barred owl pellets was found in several years of possibly an aseasonal study in [Ann Arbor, Michigan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ann_Arbor,_Michigan "Ann Arbor, Michigan") where of 777 prey items, 83.3% were meadow voles.<sup id="cite_ref-Wilson_142-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wilson-142">[142]</a></sup> At Edwin S. George Preserve near the [University of Michigan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Michigan "University of Michigan"), the summer diet was also heavily rodent based, as among 146 prey items 37.9% were white-footed mice, 22.6% were [southern bog lemming](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southern_bog_lemming "Southern bog lemming") and 6.84% were meadow voles.<sup id="cite_ref-Hamerstrom_143-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hamerstrom-143">[143]</a></sup> In a somewhat larger Michigan study, the [North American deermouse](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_American_deermouse "North American deermouse"), lead the prey at 34.9% of 321 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Errington_144-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Errington-144">[144]</a></sup> In [Minnesota](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minnesota "Minnesota"), the barred owl was counted as one of the leading causes of mortality of [prairie voles](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prairie_vole "Prairie vole").<sup id="cite_ref-145"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Studies of the barred owl diet in 6 urban [metropolitan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metropolitan_area "Metropolitan area") areas of [British Columbia](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Columbia "British Columbia") found that the diet was dominated by young rats of the invasive _[Rattus](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rattus "Rattus")_ genus, comprising 52.8% of 688 prey items, well ahead of native [Townsend's voles](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Townsend%27s_vole "Townsend's vole"), which were secondary at 19.2% of the diet. The average weight rats taken by owls were clearly juveniles, estimated to average 103 g (3.6 oz), although several could be anywhere from infant rat to adult rat sizes, i.e. about 25 to 300 g (0.88 to 10.58 oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Hindmarch_146-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hindmarch-146">[146]</a></sup> The mean size of [black rats](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_rat "Black rat") taken in Oregon was 250 g (8.8 oz), indicating that here large adults of this species were selected.<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup> Beyond the typical more meadow-dwelling voles and woodland edge-dwelling native mice, larger and more forest dwelling rodents of different varieties can be of variable import.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-16"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hamer_138-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hamer-138">[138]</a></sup> Numerous [woodrat](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pack_rat "Pack rat") species may be taken and may provide a hearty meal to a barred owl, at a mean body mass when taken (in [Oregon](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oregon "Oregon")) of 285 g (10.1 oz) for unidentified species.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>

In different areas, barred owls may regularly hunt the diverse members of the [squirrel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Squirrel "Squirrel") family, despite their general penchant for [diurnality](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diurnality "Diurnality"). Smaller squirrel varieties are usually focused on when hunted as supplement prey, such as [chipmunks](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chipmunk "Chipmunk"), averaging about 83 g (2.9 oz) among the different species they prey upon, and [pine squirrels](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pine_squirrel "Pine squirrel"), which average about twice as large as chipmunks.<sup id="cite_ref-Takats_100-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Takats-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-5"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Merritt_149-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Merritt-149">[149]</a></sup> Usually juvenile specimens are focused on when taking the larger _[Sciurus](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sciurus "Sciurus")_ [tree squirrels](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tree_squirrel "Tree squirrel"), at least in summer, but presumably a mixture of yearling and adult _Sciurus_ will be taken during winter.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-6"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup> The mean weight of [western gray squirrels](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_gray_squirrel "Western gray squirrel") taken during the breeding season in Oregon was 450 g (16 oz), against a mean adult weight of around 770 g (1.70 lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> The issue of temporal activities is less pertinent to the predator of [flying squirrels](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flying_squirrel "Flying squirrel"), which are nocturnal. All studies of the diet of barred owls in [Pacific Northwest](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacific_Northwest "Pacific Northwest") show the importance of the [northern flying squirrel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_flying_squirrel "Northern flying squirrel") to their diet. This flying squirrel was found to comprise from about 10.9% to 20% of the diet of barred owls (either as the most or second most important prey species) and, with a mean weight of 134 g (4.7 oz) when taken, they comprised up to 25.6% of the food [biomass](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biomass_(ecology) "Biomass (ecology)") for this owl species.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-7"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-5"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hamer_138-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hamer-138">[138]</a></sup> In [Green Ridge State Forest](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Green_Ridge_State_Forest "Green Ridge State Forest") in [Maryland](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maryland "Maryland"), although not numerically the most important prey family compared to unidentified cricetids and shrews, the [southern flying squirrel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southern_flying_squirrel "Southern flying squirrel") was the most often identified prey species for barred owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Devereux_151-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Devereux-151">[151]</a></sup> Beyond the aforementioned rodent prey, more infrequently rodent prey can including various other [cricetid rodents](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cricetidae "Cricetidae"), [pocket gophers](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pocket_gopher "Pocket gopher"), [mountain beavers](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mountain_beaver "Mountain beaver") (average weight when taken of up to 550 g (1.21 lb)) and [jumping mice](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jumping_mice "Jumping mice").<sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-8"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Blakemore_110-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Blakemore-110">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-6"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup> The largest known rodent prey of barred owls are adult [muskrat](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muskrat "Muskrat"), which were estimated to weigh 1,169 g (2.577 lb) when taken.<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-7"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup>

[](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Barred_owl_(Strix_varia)_dining_on_a_mole.webm "Play video")[](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Barred_owl_(Strix_varia)_dining_on_a_mole.webm)

A barred owl consuming a mole (possibly a [coast mole](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coast_mole "Coast mole")) in [Washington](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Washington_(state) "Washington (state)").

The other primary mammalian prey types are the [shrews](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shrew "Shrew") and the [moles](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mole_(animal) "Mole (animal)"). At least a dozen species of shrew and most North American species of mole are known as prey of the barred owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Sutherland_124-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Sutherland-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-6"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-9"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-8"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup> 12.8% of 7077 total prey items from across the range were [shrews or moles](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eulipotyphla "Eulipotyphla").<sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-7"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup> A small sample of prey in [Michigan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michigan "Michigan") was led by the very small [masked shrew](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Masked_shrew "Masked shrew"), which weighs around 4 g (0.14 oz), at 24% of 34 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Merritt_149-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Merritt-149">[149]</a></sup> A much larger shrew, the [northern short-tailed shrew](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_short-tailed_shrew "Northern short-tailed shrew") at around 21 g (0.74 oz), was the leading prey in [Glenwood, Minnesota](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glenwood,_Minnesota "Glenwood, Minnesota") at 36% of 81 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Blakemore_110-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Blakemore-110">[110]</a></sup> This prey species also is taken quite regularly in several other parts of the range, as well as a [closely-related species](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southern_short-tailed_shrew "Southern short-tailed shrew").<sup id="cite_ref-Devereux_151-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Devereux-151">[151]</a></sup> Assorted other shrew species and the smallest of the world's moles, the [American shrew mole](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_shrew_mole "American shrew mole") are regular supplement prey elsewhere, especially in the Pacific Northwest.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-10"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-9"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hamer_138-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hamer-138">[138]</a></sup> The most frequently taken single prey species through the Pacific Northwest, at 11.8% of 4299 total prey items of barred owls, was the 56 g (2.0 oz) [coast mole](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coast_mole "Coast mole").<sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-11"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup> Usually, moles are secondary if relatively hearty prey elsewhere in the range.<sup id="cite_ref-Whiklo2_152-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Whiklo2-152">[152]</a></sup>

Secondary prey can include several species of [cottontail rabbits](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cottontail_rabbit "Cottontail rabbit") (_Sylvilagus_ sp.) and [snowshoe hares](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Snowshoe_hare "Snowshoe hare") (_Lepus americanus_).<sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-12"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> 3.2% of 7077 prey items from across the range for barred owls were [rabbits or hares](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagomorpha "Lagomorpha").<sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-8"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup> Among cottontails, small juveniles are largely taken, but rabbits as large as an adult [brush rabbits](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brush_rabbit "Brush rabbit") can be taken.<sup id="cite_ref-Errington_144-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Errington-144">[144]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-10"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup> The weights of snowshoe hares taken by barred owls in the Pacific Northwest were estimated at ranging from 50 g (0.11 lb) leverets to juveniles and standard-sized adults about 1,200 to 1,400 g (2.6 to 3.1 lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-13"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-11"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hamer_138-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hamer-138">[138]</a></sup>

[Bats](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bat "Bat") are infrequently reported as prey in most of the range but an unusually close association was detected in [Valdosta, Georgia](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Valdosta,_Georgia "Valdosta, Georgia"), where most of the prey, 65% of pellet contents and 37 total bats, were [southeastern myotis](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southeastern_myotis "Southeastern myotis") (_Myotis austroriparius_).<sup id="cite_ref-154"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> Barred owls are also known predators of small mammalian [carnivorans](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carnivora "Carnivora"), mainly [mustelids](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mustelid "Mustelid") such as [stoats](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stoat "Stoat") (_Mustela erminea_) and [long-tailed weasels](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Long-tailed_weasel "Long-tailed weasel") (_Neogale frenata_).<sup id="cite_ref-155"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Predation on small [American mink](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_mink "American mink") (_Neogale vison_) have also been reported.<sup id="cite_ref-Errington_144-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Errington-144">[144]</a></sup> As they are seemingly not affected by strong odor, [eastern spotted](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_spotted_skunk "Eastern spotted skunk") and [western spotted skunks](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_spotted_skunk "Western spotted skunk") (_Spilogale gracilis_ & _S. putorius_) of all ages can be taken.<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-12"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> Much larger mammals are sometimes recorded in the foods of barred owls, but there are few details known about the age, condition, or circumstances (i.e. they may have been consumed as [carrion](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carrion "Carrion") or, perhaps more likely, young or infirm specimens were taken).<sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-9"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup> Some such prey species recorded have included the [Virginia opossum](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virginia_opossum "Virginia opossum"), the [North American porcupine](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_American_porcupine "North American porcupine"),<sup id="cite_ref-Marks_134-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Marks-134">[134]</a></sup> the [striped skunk](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Striped_skunk "Striped skunk")<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-15"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup> and the [domestic cat](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cat "Cat"). Adults of all these species are known to count amongst the prey of great horned owls which are better suited than barred owls to take particularly large prey given its more robust morphology.<sup id="cite_ref-159"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup>

### Birds [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Barred_owl&action=edit&section=11 "Edit section: Birds")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hermit_thrush_(27991992249).jpg)

Studies have determined that [hermit thrush](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hermit_thrush "Hermit thrush") cannot nest successfully in close proximity to barred owls due to their predatory pressure.

Throughout the barred owl's range, other birds are taken as prey, although avian species make up a much smaller proportion of their diets than mammals. The maximum known representation of bird prey in a barred owl food study was 25.1% in [Alberta](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alberta "Alberta"), meaning that they augment their diet less heavily with bird prey than their near equivalent in Europe, the [tawny owl](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tawny_owl "Tawny owl").<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-17"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> No specific variety of bird is subject to the most frequent predation by barred owls and birds are the most diverse class in these owl's prey spectrum, with more than 100 species of bird known to be hunted.<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-6"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-10"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-14"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup> Conspicuous nesting sites of [barn swallow](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barn_swallow "Barn swallow") and [purple martin](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Purple_martin "Purple martin") on manmade structures and objects were revealed via video-monitoring to suffer heavy predation by barred owls. In the case of the barn swallows, all ages of swallow as well as possibly eggs were eaten. 65 of 95 monitoring barn swallow nests were consumed by owls over a 3-year period.<sup id="cite_ref-162"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> The [muddy bank](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bank_(geography) "Bank (geography)") nests of [cliff swallows](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_cliff_swallow "American cliff swallow") are also vulnerable to barred owls, while other swallow species are known to be opportunistically taken.<sup id="cite_ref-164"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>

In more enclosed wooded areas, radio-tagging and [video-monitoring](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Camera_trap "Camera trap") of various passerines nests as well as examinations of owl pellets has shed light on the relationship of barred owls with these potential prey resources. Not only was the barred owl found to be a surprisingly routine predator at woodland passerine nests, but that an unexpected bulk of the acts of predation in studies from Missouri and Illinois were carried out during the daytime. Many different forest bird species (most frequently [acadian flycatchers](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acadian_flycatcher "Acadian flycatcher") and [indigo buntings](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indigo_bunting "Indigo bunting") in Missouri and Illinois) were hunted. These studies indicated that the barred owl may snatch passerines of any age, but recent fledglings are taken preferentially due to their more conspicuous behavior and limited ability to fly away. In Minnesota, about 62% of studied [hermit thrush](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hermit_thrush "Hermit thrush") and [ovenbird](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ovenbird "Ovenbird") fledglings were taken per one study, with all thrush that nested in the 50 m (160 ft) radius of the barred owl's nests failing to produce any young. A similarly high rate of local determent by barred owls has been found for other woodland thrushes like the [veery](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Veery "Veery"), [wood thrush](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wood_thrush "Wood thrush") and [varied thrush](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Varied_thrush "Varied thrush"), with the additional finding that pre-dawn singing by certain thrushes, when their escape abilities are dulled by the dim light, leaves them vulnerable to barred owl ambushes.<sup id="cite_ref-Takats_100-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Takats-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gill_165-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Gill-165">[165]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Streby_166-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Streby-166">[166]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-167"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> Forest birds seem to recognize the barred owl as a threat, with mobbing behavior evoked easily by playing recordings of their calls in the daytime.

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ruffed_Grouse_(1).jpg)

Ruffed grouse are known prey in extensive parts of the range.

A wide diversity of bird prey may be occasionally hunted by barred owls in different circumstances. Smaller or mid-sized bird prey species known have including different species, though usually a relatively low species diversity and in low numbers, beyond swallows and thrushes of [tyrant flycatchers](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tyrant_flycatcher "Tyrant flycatcher"), [vireos](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vireo "Vireo"), [chickadees](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chickadee "Chickadee"), [wrens](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wren "Wren"), [mimids](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mimid "Mimid"), [tanagers](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Piranga "Piranga"), other [cardinalids](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardinalidae "Cardinalidae") and [finches](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Finches "Finches").<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-7"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-13"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup> Somewhat higher diversity of species are known from the [sparrow](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_sparrow "American sparrow") and [warbler](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_World_warbler "New World warbler") families.<sup id="cite_ref-Gill_165-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Gill-165">[165]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> Birds down to the size of the [calliope hummingbird](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calliope_hummingbird "Calliope hummingbird"), North America's smallest hummingbird at 2.7 g (0.095 oz), may be taken by barred owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-15"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup> At the opposite end of passerine prey for barred owls, this species will sometimes take all ages of the [American crow](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_crow "American crow"), from very young nestlings to adults.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-18"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup> Numerous non-passerine birds are also taken, though seldom in great numbers and of low known species diversity.<sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-11"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup> One exceptional family is the [woodpeckers](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Woodpecker "Woodpecker"), which are probably so widely taken because of their generally overlapping habitat preferences with those of barred owls. Several species of woodpecker are preyed upon almost throughout the range, including at least a half dozen in Oregon alone, from the smallest North American species, the [downy woodpecker](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Downy_woodpecker "Downy woodpecker"), to the largest, the [pileated woodpecker](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pileated_woodpecker "Pileated woodpecker").<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-14"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fisher_170-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Fisher-170">[170]</a></sup> Other small-to-medium-sized bird species known as prey for barred owls are: [mountain quail](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mountain_quail "Mountain quail"), [grey partridge](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grey_partridge "Grey partridge"), [rock dove](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rock_dove "Rock dove"), [band-tailed pigeon](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Band-tailed_pigeon "Band-tailed pigeon"), [mourning dove](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mourning_dove "Mourning dove"), [purple gallinule](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_purple_gallinule "American purple gallinule"), [killdeer](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Killdeer "Killdeer"), [American woodcock](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_woodcock "American woodcock"), [least tern](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Least_tern "Least tern"), [snowy egret](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Snowy_egret "Snowy egret"),<sup id="cite_ref-171"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> [cattle egret](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cattle_egret "Cattle egret")<sup id="cite_ref-172"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> and [belted kingfisher](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Belted_kingfisher "Belted kingfisher"). Although they take many chicks of gamebirds, adults of these species are vulnerable as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-8"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup> In many areas, [ruffed grouse](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ruffed_grouse "Ruffed grouse") are not infrequently taken, comprising up to nearly 6% of prey items in Alberta. In Oregon, the weights estimated for ruffed grouse taken by barred owls varied enormously, from small chicks estimated at 25 g (0.88 oz), to adults weighing about 576 g (1.270 lb). Broader study in the Pacific Northwest indicated that adult ruffed grouse were mainly taken.<sup id="cite_ref-Takats_100-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Takats-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-16"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-15"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> Barred owls are also known to take adult [spruce grouse](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spruce_grouse "Spruce grouse") of about the same size as the ruffed grouse, as well as much larger species including the [common pheasant](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_pheasant "Common pheasant") and the [sooty grouse](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sooty_grouse "Sooty grouse"), the latter estimated to average 1,050 g (2.31 lb) when taken.<sup id="cite_ref-Marks_134-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Marks-134">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-16"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Barred owls are also known to prey on the young of other, larger birds, such as the [American white ibis](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_white_ibis "American white ibis") and [wild turkey](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wild_turkey "Wild turkey").<sup id="cite_ref-175"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup>

### Other prey [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Barred_owl&action=edit&section=12 "Edit section: Other prey")

Barred owl predation on reptiles is widely reported but they seldom take large numbers in any given area.<sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-12"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup> Most reported instances of such captures are of various small [lizards](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lizard "Lizard"), often of [skinks](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Skink "Skink") in the genus _[Plestiodon](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plestiodon "Plestiodon"),_ most often from the [Midwest](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Midwest "Midwest") to the western parts of the range.<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-9"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Blakemore_110-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Blakemore-110">[110]</a></sup> Several reported instances of barred owls hunting [snakes](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Snake "Snake") are also known, but they are perhaps even more seldom preyed upon than lizards.<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-10"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-17"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup> More than a half dozen snake species are known to be captured, several of which are [colubrids](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colubridae "Colubridae"), which are mostly harmless.<sup id="cite_ref-177"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-178"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> The estimated body mass of [black racer](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coluber_constrictor "Coluber constrictor") taken in Oregon was only 77 g (2.7 oz), well under their mean mature size.<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-18"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup> However, consumption or predation on dangerous [pit vipers](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pit_viper "Pit viper"), such as [timber rattlesnakes](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timber_rattlesnake "Timber rattlesnake") and [copperheads](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Agkistrodon_contortrix "Agkistrodon contortrix"), by barred owls has been reported, although it is not known whether these are taken as adults.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-16"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-11"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> Rarer still is barred owl predation on [turtles](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turtle "Turtle").<sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-13"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup> Predation by this species was reported upon a very young [river cooter](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/River_cooter "River cooter"), which had a carapace width of only 31.4 mm (1.24 in), as well as on juvenile [gopher tortoise](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gopher_tortoise "Gopher tortoise") and apparently [diamondback terrapin](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diamondback_terrapin "Diamondback terrapin").<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-12"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> Much more characteristic than any reptilian prey are [amphibians](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amphibian "Amphibian"), with various types of [frogs](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frog "Frog"), [salamanders](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salamander "Salamander") and similar species reported in this owl's foods.<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-13"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-14"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup> Amphibians were considerably more popular in the diet in western part of range, comprising 10.5% of known studies against 4.4% in the east.<sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-15"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup> In total, well over 20 amphibian species have been identified in the foods of barred owls and amphibians collectively can make up to 24.5% of the local diet (as was reported in Alberta).<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-19"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Takats_100-5"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Takats-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-16"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup> While salamanders and newts are probably often visually discerned while scanning the forest floor, many frogs are probably hunted down by sound during their crepuscular choruses.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_65-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Johnsgard-65">[65]</a></sup>

Amphibians are taken almost entirely during the breeding season, as they become unavailable to barred owls during the winter months.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-17"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-19"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup> Most reported amphibian prey in Oregon were unidentified "medium-sized salamanders". A diversity of frog sizes may be taken, varying potentially in size from [spring peepers](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spring_peeper "Spring peeper"), which average around 4 g (0.14 oz), to [American bullfrogs](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_bullfrog "American bullfrog"), which average around in mature bullfrogs 430 g (15 oz).<sup id="cite_ref-183"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> A notable act of successful predation was carried out by a barred owl on a [rough-skinned newt](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rough-skinned_newt "Rough-skinned newt"), which contains [toxins](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toxin "Toxin") that are often deadly to predators. Apparently, the owl was able to survive after consuming the newt.<sup id="cite_ref-185"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> The rarest variety of vertebrate prey for barred owls is fish.<sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-17"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup> However, there are several accrued accounts of fishing by these owls, including older accounts of barred owls coming to [ice holes](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_fishing "Ice fishing") made by human [fishermen](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fishermen "Fishermen") and more recent accounts of possibly routine fishing by the owls on the [St. Johns River](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/St._Johns_River "St. Johns River") in Florida, in the latter case utilizing a fishing [dock](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dock_(maritime) "Dock (maritime)") as a hunting perch.<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-14"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> At least five species of fish have been identified in the foods of barred owls, including fairly large fish like [brown bullheads](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brown_bullhead "Brown bullhead") and [largemouth bass](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Largemouth_bass "Largemouth bass").<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-17"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-15"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-20"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Wood_Frog_-_Lithobates_sylvaticus,_Lake_Accotink_Park,_Springfield,_Virginia_(39440713811).jpg)

Frogs such as [wood frogs](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wood_frog "Wood frog") are a regular prey source for barred owls.

The balance of assorted [invertebrates](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Invertebrate "Invertebrate") in the diet of barred owls can be quite high. Although many of those found in pellets are unidentified to species, nearly 40 species of invertebrates have been found in their foods.<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-16"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-18"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-21"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup> Broad studies indicate [arthropods](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arthropod "Arthropod") (including [millipedes](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Millipede "Millipede") and [spiders](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spider "Spider") but predominantly [insects](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Insect "Insect")) in general comprise up to about 16% of the barred owl's foods.<sup id="cite_ref-Snyder_133-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Snyder-133">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-18"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup> When hunting [insects](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Insect "Insect"), barred owls most often prefer ground-based [beetles](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beetle "Beetle"). In Oregon, 11.7% of the diet was assorted beetles (14 species were identified), being somewhat more numerous among 3686 prey items than other non-rodent prey orders.<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-22"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup> However, some insects prey such as [luna moth](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luna_moth "Luna moth"), [eastern tiger swallowtail](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Papilio_glaucus "Papilio glaucus") and [green darner](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Green_darner "Green darner") are presumably [hawked](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hawking_(birds) "Hawking (birds)") on the wing around dawn and dusk.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-18"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-19"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup> It is known that barred owls will sometimes come hunt near manmade light sources and [campfires](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Campfire "Campfire"), flying out from the perch to quickly capture flying insects that were attracted to them.<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-17"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fisher_170-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Fisher-170">[170]</a></sup> Of 123 prey items found in southern Manitoba, flying [Sphinx moths](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sphingidae "Sphingidae") and flightless [scarab beetles](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phyllophaga "Phyllophaga") each comprised 7% (most of the remaining balance being unidentified mammals and birds). [Crayfish](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crayfish "Crayfish") of at least four identified species are another widely taken type of invertebrate, presumably snatched up by barred owls from shallow waterways.<sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-20"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-23"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Blakemore_110-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Blakemore-110">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-187"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> Crayfish seem to be exclusively taken in the east and Midwest, areas where they comprised 3.4% of all known food studies, and none at all were recorded in the western part of the range.<sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-21"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup> In [Missouri](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Missouri "Missouri"), annually up to 31.1% (though, in some years, 0%) of the diet was comprised by crayfish. Barred owls occasionally feed on [snails](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Snail "Snail") and [slugs](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slug "Slug"), the latter especially in the Pacific Northwest.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-31"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Konig-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Worm_131-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Worm-131">[131]</a></sup> Another snail, the [Pacific sideband](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monadenia_fidelis "Monadenia fidelis"), was surprisingly often present in the foods in Oregon as at least 135 were taken.<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-24"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup> Beyond slugs, other "soft" invertebrates are sometimes hunted, especially [earthworms](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earthworm "Earthworm"). Earthworms were most prominent in the foods of barred owls in [Nova Scotia](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nova_Scotia "Nova Scotia"), where 27.6% of 186 video-monitored prey deliveries in Nova Scotia were worms, the most regularly delivered of all prey types there.<sup id="cite_ref-Elderkin_127-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Elderkin-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Worm_131-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Worm-131">[131]</a></sup>

### Interspecies predatory relationships [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Barred_owl&action=edit&section=13 "Edit section: Interspecies predatory relationships")

The barred owl's range overlaps with multiple other predators of similar prey species. Due to the time period barred owls are active, the most interaction occurs with other owl species. Of the owls in North America, about three-quarters are reliant on similar small mammal prey, usually rodents, with a mixture of other prey genera as supplements.<sup id="cite_ref-Weidensaul_23-6"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Weidensaul-23">[23]</a></sup> Other than its ecology where it today co-exists with spotted owls, however, there is some level of [niche differentiation](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Niche_differentiation "Niche differentiation") from a majority of sympatric owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-19"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup> In the eastern forest biome, the barred owl is the only large owl species to dwell mainly in continuous forest areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-20"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-20"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti_136-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Marti-136">[136]</a></sup> While many of the owls sympatric with barred owls over majority of their eastern and Midwestern range share a preference for hole-nesting, smaller hole-nesting owls usually prefer different habitats, such as the open country-dwelling [barn owl](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barn_owl "Barn owl") and the [screech owls](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Screech_owls "Screech owls"), which usually in North America dwell at the interface of forest and open habitats.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-21"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_65-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Johnsgard-65">[65]</a></sup> Both North American species in the _[Aegolius](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aegolius "Aegolius")_ owl genus are forest-dwelling cavity nesters but are much smaller and are at entirely different [trophic levels](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trophic_level "Trophic level").<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-22"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_65-5"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Johnsgard-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Grossman_104-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Grossman-104">[104]</a></sup>

A singular diurnal raptor species that mirrors the barred owl at nearly all ecological levels is the [red-shouldered hawk](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red-shouldered_hawk "Red-shouldered hawk"). Both species have similar distributions, habitat preferences and somewhat similar dietary habits and trophic level. They may considered as nearly nocturnal and diurnal [ecological equivalents](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecological_niche "Ecological niche").<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-21"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> In multiple parts of the range, including southwestern Ohio, North Carolina and northern Michigan, the paralleling habitat usage and nesting behavior of the barred owl and red-shouldered hawk has been noted. One of the few respects in which their habitat usage differs is that the barred owl is more adaptive to nesting in suburban areas if they have old growth trees that provide plentiful [tree hollows](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tree_hollow "Tree hollow"). In contrast, red-shouldered hawks tend to avoid suburban areas whilst nesting, but may acclimate to these areas during the winter. Barred owls seldom alter their range throughout the year and remain more or less constrained to the stands they used during nesting.<sup id="cite_ref-Dykstra_95-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Dykstra-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-189"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Postupalsky_190-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Postupalsky-190">[190]</a></sup> The red-shouldered hawk averages somewhat smaller and has a more limited diet than barred owls, but probably goes after dangerous prey such as snakes more regularly.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-23"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-18"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> Multiple occasions are recorded where the barred owl and red-shouldered hawk have nested in the same area, often within the same grove of trees, remarkably with little to no conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-22"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_65-6"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Johnsgard-65">[65]</a></sup> At least one nest was found including eggs from both species.<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-19"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup> When nesting near other hawks like [red-tailed hawks](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red-tailed_hawk "Red-tailed hawk") and [Cooper's hawks](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cooper%27s_hawk "Cooper's hawk"), the relationship tends to be much more contentious between hawk and owl, despite barred owls sometimes sharing space or using the old nests of these hawks.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-24"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup> A wing-clapping display by a pair of barred owls was recorded during antagonistic encounters in Manitoba with a pair of [broad-winged hawks](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Broad-winged_hawk "Broad-winged hawk"). Wing-claps were previously not recorded in this species, and when recorded for other owl species were often for the purposes of courtship displays.<sup id="cite_ref-192"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Great_Horned_Owl_on_Seedskadee_National_Wildlife_Refuge_(22312850906).jpg)

[Great horned owls](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_horned_owl "Great horned owl") are the most severe competitor and predator of barred owls.

One predator that is a major source of conflict for the barred owl is the [great horned owl](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_horned_owl "Great horned owl").<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-23"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup> In every part of their range, barred owls are compelled to share space with the larger owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-25"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup> There is habitat partitioning between the barred owl and great horned owl that allows them to often co-exist. Great horned owls prefer various more open habitats mixed with trees, often in rather upland areas, which differs from the habitats preferred by the barred owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-26"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_65-7"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Johnsgard-65">[65]</a></sup> If a great horned owl moves into an area, barred owls appear to avoid said areas, based on radio telemetry data.<sup id="cite_ref-Laidig_85-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Laidig-85">[85]</a></sup> In much of the east, habitat alteration and fragmentation tends to favor the great horned owl at the expense of the barred owl. Where more continuous forest is available, however, the great horned and barred owl can occur surprisingly close to one another. In one case, a barred owl was observed to roost only 400 m (1,300 ft) from a great horned owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Laidig_85-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Laidig-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> In general the [reforestation](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reforestation "Reforestation") at the northern sections of the [Great Plains](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Plains "Great Plains") have, on the contrary, benefited barred owls (and may have been a part of allowing their westward expansion) and been perhaps slightly detrimental to the great horned owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Holt_69-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Holt-69">[69]</a></sup> However, even where the habitat becomes less ideal, the great horned owl is unlikely to vacate an area, unlike the barred owl which can be entirely displaced if woods become too small and fragmented.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-24"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Duncan_70-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Duncan-70">[70]</a></sup> Great horned owls and barred owls have similar diets, as both are wide-ranging, large and opportunistic owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Weidensaul_23-7"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Weidensaul-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_65-8"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Johnsgard-65">[65]</a></sup> However, the great horned owl is larger in bulk, sometimes averaging nearly twice as heavy, with much heavier, larger feet and talons.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-27"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Snyder_133-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Snyder-133">[133]</a></sup> The great horned owl has a more powerful grip strength<sup id="cite_ref-Ward_128-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Ward-128">[128]</a></sup> and access to a wider variety of prey than barred owls, which take fewer prey species because they occur in more limited habitats and have a smaller overall distribution.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-28"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-20"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti_136-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Marti-136">[136]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Earhart_40-6"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Earhart-40">[40]</a></sup> A study utilizing [stable isotopes](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stable_isotope_ratio "Stable isotope ratio") in Alberta appeared to confirm that the great horned owl preys on nearly twice as many prey species as do the barred owls of the area.<sup id="cite_ref-195"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup>

The great horned owl is indeed likely to be the greatest natural enemy of the barred owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-29"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-21"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup> There are several accounts of the horned owl species preying on nestlings, fledglings and adults of the barred.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-25"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-22"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fuller_91-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Fuller-91">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> Other than horned owls, predation of the barred owl seems to be fairly rare outside of the nestling age, both due to the owl's relatively large size and their nesting habits, most often in secluded tree hollows.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-26"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup> One more virulent nest predator is likely to be [raccoons](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Raccoon "Raccoon") (_Procyon lotor_), which can nearly decimate both tree hollow and stick nest locations of almost any kind of bird, especially in [peri-urban](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peri-urbanisation "Peri-urbanisation") areas where they largely exist without controlling predators.<sup id="cite_ref-Elderkin_127-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Elderkin-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-197"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> Both the [American marten](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_marten "American marten") and the [fisher](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fisher_(animal) "Fisher (animal)") are known to be nest predators of barred owls, in turn appearing to cause the owls to switch nest sites.<sup id="cite_ref-198"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> Other birds of prey may be an occasional threat to barred owls. [Northern goshawks](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_goshawk "Northern goshawk") have reportedly killed both young and adult barred owls.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-27"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-23"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup> One modern account mentions predation by a goshawk on a nestling barred owl (i.e. weight about 392 g (13.8 oz)).<sup id="cite_ref-199"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> A well-known instance of a goshawk attack on an adult barred owl concluded with the owl and goshawk killing one another.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-30"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> There are some very rare, singular cases of predation on barred owls (age unknown) by [red-tailed hawks](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red-tailed_hawk "Red-tailed hawk"), which nonetheless generally appears to be a less menacing co-inhabitant than the goshawk.<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-24"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shupe_201-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Shupe-201">[201]</a></sup> More surprisingly, a [Cooper's hawk](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cooper%27s_hawk "Cooper's hawk"), a smaller species of raptor, was observed to apparently prey upon a full-grown barred owl in British Columbia.<sup id="cite_ref-202"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> An [American alligator](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_alligator "American alligator") was reported to have preyed on a barred owl in at least one instance.<sup id="cite_ref-203"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup>

On the other hand, the barred owl is a significant predator of smaller raptor species as well. Most smaller owls that co-exist with barred owls are at occasional risk of predation.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-31"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup> A particular cause of concern in intraguild predations<sup>[<i><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="You can help -- (July 2020)">definition needed</span></a></i>]</sup> by the barred has been their encroachment into the areas inhabited by [western screech owl](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_screech_owl "Western screech owl") (_Megascops kennicotti_). Several instances of predation have been noted on the screech owls and the recent, unprecedented presence of the barred owls appears to have a correlation with the decline of the western screech owl in British Columbia and in [Bainbridge Island, Washington](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bainbridge_Island,_Washington "Bainbridge Island, Washington").<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-25"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-205"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> In one instance, a biologist who called in an [eastern screech owl](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_screech_owl "Eastern screech owl") (_Megascops asio_) in Ohio observed it to be immediately captured and consumed by a barred owl. The barred owl is a serious predator of eastern screech owls, but is less deadly to them in general than the great horned owl.<sup id="cite_ref-206"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> Various additional owl species known to be preyed upon by barred owls including the [flammulated owl](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flammulated_owl "Flammulated owl") (_Psiloscops flammeolus_),<sup id="cite_ref-207"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> [northern pygmy owl](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_pygmy_owl "Northern pygmy owl") (_Glaucidium gnoma_),<sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-19"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup> [northern saw-whet owl](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_saw-whet_owl "Northern saw-whet owl") (_Aegolius acadius_)<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_137-26"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Graham-137">[137]</a></sup> and [long-eared owl](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Long-eared_owl "Long-eared owl") (_Asio otus_).<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-25"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup> The interspecific owl trophic chain was perhaps most conspicuous in a case where a barred owl that was shot in New England was found to have a long-eared owl inside its stomach which in turn had an eastern screech-owl in its own stomach.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-32"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-26"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup> More unexpectedly, barred owls may even prey on other _Strix_ species. As many as four records of circumstantial but likely predation by barred owls on [spotted owls](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spotted_owl "Spotted owl") have been reported.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-20"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup> A likely event of predation by a barred owl on an adult [great grey owl](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_grey_owl "Great grey owl") was observed. The authors hypothesized that the victim may have been a smaller male great grey owl (which can be about the same body mass as a large female barred) but this is the only known instance to date of a great gray owl being killed by another species of _Strix_ owl.<sup id="cite_ref-208"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> Barred owls have been known to take adults (or full-grown juveniles) of diurnal raptors as well, including [snail kites](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Snail_kite "Snail kite"), [Cooper's hawks](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cooper%27s_hawk "Cooper's hawk"), [sharp-shinned hawks](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sharp-shinned_hawk "Sharp-shinned hawk") and possibly [swallow-tailed kites](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Swallow-tailed_kite "Swallow-tailed kite").<sup id="cite_ref-Fisher_170-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Fisher-170">[170]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-209"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-210"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-211"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup>

## Reproduction [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Barred_owl&action=edit&section=14 "Edit section: Reproduction")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Two-owls-perched-branch_-_West_Virginia_-_ForestWander.jpg)

A probable mated pair of barred owl adults in daytime

The barred owl's preferred nest site is usually the [hollow trunk](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tree_hollow "Tree hollow") of a large tree or the broken-off [snag](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Snag_(ecology) "Snag (ecology)") from a large tree branch. Hollows or snags may be made by any variety of sources, often due to a [disease](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plant_pathology "Plant pathology") or storm, with hollows and snags large enough to accommodate these birds usually only occurring in an old-growth tree.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_26-11"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hume-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-27"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Peck_60-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Peck-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Devereux_151-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Devereux-151">[151]</a></sup> Typically, nest sites are in rather deep and dark wooded areas, often with a well-developed understory but somewhat sparse lower branches, and may be fairly close to water.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-32"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Konig-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Applegate_111-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Applegate-111">[111]</a></sup> Average nest heights are between 6.8 and 13.4 m (22 and 44 ft) above the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-28"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup> In 25 studies from throughout the range, mean nest tree height was 18.2 m (60 ft) (lowest mean from Florida where it was 5.9 m (19 ft); the highest from Oregon where it was 28 m (92 ft)), mean nest tree diameter was 65.7 cm (25.9 in) (thinnest mean in Saskatchewan at 47.4 cm (18.7 in); thickest in Washington at 106 cm (42 in)), while the mean height of the tree cavity used from these studies was 9.8 m (32 ft).<sup id="cite_ref-Livezey5_116-22"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Livezey5-116">[116]</a></sup>

A big [beech](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beech "Beech") with a rotting core, encompassing a large cavity reached through a deep crevice, is an ideal nest site.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_26-12"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hume-26">[26]</a></sup> The most widely reported nesting trees in breeding cards were [elms](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elm "Elm") (21%) and beeches (15%), followed by [oaks](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oak "Oak"), [hickories](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hickory "Hickory"), [yellow birches](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Betula_alleghaniensis "Betula alleghaniensis"), [sycamores](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platanus_occidentalis "Platanus occidentalis"), [aspens](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aspen "Aspen"), [maples](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maple "Maple") and [poplars](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populus "Populus").<sup id="cite_ref-Apfelbaum_212-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Apfelbaum-212">[212]</a></sup> In Washington, the most often used nest trees were [balsam poplar](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populus_balsamifera "Populus balsamifera"), [Douglas fir](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pseudotsuga_menziesii "Pseudotsuga menziesii") and [grand fir](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abies_grandis "Abies grandis").<sup id="cite_ref-Buchanan_10-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Buchanan-10">[10]</a></sup> Balsam poplar comprised 62% of known nest sites in Manitoba as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Whiklo2_152-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Whiklo2-152">[152]</a></sup> It was found in Maryland that snags used were significantly higher than the average height of miscellaneous snags available in environment.
