The mean size of tree holes the owls used in Maryland was 33 cm (13 in) in inside diameter and 54 cm (21 in) in depth.<sup id="cite_ref-Devereux_151-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Devereux-151">[151]</a></sup> These were slightly larger than the average from Michigan, which hollow used averaged 25 cm (9.8 in) in mean diameter and 35 cm (14 in) in mean depth.<sup id="cite_ref-Postupalsky_190-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Postupalsky-190">[190]</a></sup> Record depth tree cavities used by barred owls was around 2.4 m (7.9 ft) deep from the opening on the tree.<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-28"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup> One study of cavity nesting birds in Ontario found that the barred owl preferred to nest in the most massive trees of any cavity-nester in the study area.<sup id="cite_ref-213"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> In continuous bottomland forest, nests are often about every 226 ha (560 acres).<sup id="cite_ref-214"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> Territory lines often remain the same even after the original owls are replaced entirely by a new pair.<sup id="cite_ref-Nicholls_82-6"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Nicholls-82">[82]</a></sup> It was thought in Nova Scotia that some pairs may prospect a potential nest site as much as year before they use it.<sup id="cite_ref-Elderkin_127-5"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Elderkin-127">[127]</a></sup> Despite usually using sickly or dying trees, some nests have found in partially hollow but still living [oaks](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oak "Oak").<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-33"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Konig-4">[4]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Barred-owl-chick-17b.jpg)

Barred owl nestlings peer out of the typical nesting site, a spacious tree hollow.

Usually in areas with few or no natural tree hollows, often within younger [secondary forests](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Secondary_forest "Secondary forest") or [overharvested](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Overexploitation "Overexploitation") areas, this species will uses other [birds' nests](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nest "Nest") and occasionally also the [dreys](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drey "Drey") of squirrels.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-34"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Konig-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_26-13"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hume-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Apfelbaum_212-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Apfelbaum-212">[212]</a></sup> Evidence shows that the preference of barred owls for hollows and snags over bird nests is due to their earlier nest type having a more secure [microclimate](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microclimate "Microclimate") with better shelter (additionally, owl nests in hollows generally tend to be somewhat less vulnerable to predation than those of owls using old bird nests).<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-29"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> Nest built by other birds that are most widely used are probably [red-shouldered hawk](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red-shouldered_hawk "Red-shouldered hawk") and [Cooper's hawks](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cooper%27s_hawk "Cooper's hawk"), while those of [red-tailed hawks](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red-tailed_hawk "Red-tailed hawk"), usually being in more open areas, are used secondarily outside of a local basis.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-33"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shupe_201-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Shupe-201">[201]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> In the south, nests have been found between fronds of [palmetto](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sabal "Sabal") palm leaves, in holes of broken palm stems and [rotten snags](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Snag_(ecology) "Snag (ecology)") of the palms.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-35"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Konig-4">[4]</a></sup> One unconventional nest was on the ground at the base of a lookout tower in [Everglades National Park](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Everglades_National_Park "Everglades National Park").<sup id="cite_ref-Robertson_217-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Robertson-217">[217]</a></sup> Another was on the roof of a shed in Saskatchewan.<sup id="cite_ref-218"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> Yet another was in an [earthen bank](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bank_(geography) "Bank (geography)") in Texas.<sup id="cite_ref-219"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> Locally, barred owls can take to [nest boxes](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nest_box "Nest box") but, in general, barred owls take to these less readily than their cousin _Strix_ owls in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-36"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Konig-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-220"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> Suitable nesting hollows may be used quite often in subsequent years, with records of a single hollow seeing up to 25 years of barred owl use (presumably not by the same owls however).<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-37"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Konig-4">[4]</a></sup> Other long-used nests were one reused in New England for 10 years by the same pair until it rotted out and while a nest box in Nova Scotia was reused 10 times over 16 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-30"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Elderkin_127-6"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Elderkin-127">[127]</a></sup> Over 6 years in Minnesota, 14 nest boxes were reused; 7 were used once, 6 were twice and one was used three times.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnson2_221-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Johnson2-221">[221]</a></sup> Like all owls, barred owls never construct their owl nest but they may press or dig slightly if soil is present or remove the top leaves from a squirrel [drey](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drey "Drey").<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-31"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Elderkin_127-7"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Elderkin-127">[127]</a></sup>

In normal circumstances, barred owls tend to pair bond for life.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-29"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup> Courting pairs are usually newly mature birds but also possibly widowed owls or those re-establishing existing pair bonds after the winter.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-30"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-32"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup> During courtship, barred owl males especially may engage in nodding, bowing with half-spread wings and may wobble and twist their head from side to side.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-31"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-33"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup> Courting pairs will often engage in duets.<sup id="cite_ref-222"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> [Copulation](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copulation_(zoology) "Copulation (zoology)") between pairs usually occurs in late winter, February to March, and occurs several times, probably to ensure implantation.<sup id="cite_ref-223"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> The female enters a pre-breeding lethargy stage wherein she is fed by the male.<sup id="cite_ref-Elderkin_127-8"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Elderkin-127">[127]</a></sup> In barred owls, egg laying occurs at two to three day intervals.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_26-14"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hume-26">[26]</a></sup> Egg laying typically begins in March and runs throughout April.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-32"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup> In more tropical locales like Florida, egg laying may occur as early as late December, though 22 were laid in between early January and early March.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-33"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup> At similar latitudes in Texas, 22 first eggs were laid between as early as mid-February to as late as early June (although the latter may have been a replacement clutch).<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-34"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup> 23 clutches in Iowa and Illinois were initiated in between late February and late April, with roughly corresponding initial egg dates in New Jersey and probably western Maryland as well.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-35"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bosakowski_96-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bosakowski-96">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Devereux_151-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Devereux-151">[151]</a></sup> 63 initial eggs in New England were laid between mid-March and mid-May.<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-34"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup> Further north, 38 clutches in Nova Scotia were initiated in between late March and late May, with initial clutches dates about a week later in Ontario.<sup id="cite_ref-Peck_60-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Peck-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Elderkin_127-9"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Elderkin-127">[127]</a></sup> Normally the female lays 2 to 3 eggs, although as many as 5 is possible, directly on the base of the nest site.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-38"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Konig-4">[4]</a></sup> The mean clutch size in three broad samples from several parts of the range was between 2.22 and 2.46.<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-35"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Apfelbaum_212-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Apfelbaum-212">[212]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-224"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> In Minnesota, the mean clutch size was 2.68.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnson2_221-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Johnson2-221">[221]</a></sup> The eggs are pure white, slightly rough and not glossy, and oval in shape.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-36"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup> In 157 eggs, the length of the egg could measure from 42.5 to 55.5 mm (1.67 to 2.19 in) while the diameter could range from 37.5 to 45.3 mm (1.48 to 1.78 in), with a mean of 50.6 mm × 43.3 mm (1.99 in × 1.70 in).<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-36"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup> 25 eggs in Nova Scotia averaged slightly smaller, at 49 mm × 41 mm (1.9 in × 1.6 in).<sup id="cite_ref-Elderkin_127-10"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Elderkin-127">[127]</a></sup> The mean weight of the egg is around 45.5 g (1.60 oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Elderkin_127-11"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Elderkin-127">[127]</a></sup> The female alone incubates, doing so for about 28 days, while the male gathers food for her.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_4-39"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Konig-4">[4]</a></sup>

The female tends to closely brood the young for three weeks. Then after, she begins to hunt for the young. Both parents normally continue hunt for the young until they are about 6 weeks old and can fly well.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_26-15"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hume-26">[26]</a></sup> During the first three weeks, the male does all prey deliveries, either bringing prey directly into the nest or leaves for the female at the nest entrance or on a nearby branch. The mean number of prey deliveries when the young are 6–10 days old is 2.4 per night, 1.4 when they are 11–15 days old, 3.6 when they are 16–20 days old and 2.2 when they are 21–25 days old. He will continue hunting until the young disperse.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-37"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Elderkin_127-12"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Elderkin-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Robertson_217-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Robertson-217">[217]</a></sup> The female's prey deliveries are much more frequent when she resumes hunting, ranging from a mean of 4.8 to 8 deliveries nightly.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-38"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Elderkin_127-13"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Elderkin-127">[127]</a></sup> Male prey deliveries are often sporadically at any point through the night while female deliveries tend to be clustered immediately after sunset or just before sunrise.<sup id="cite_ref-Elderkin_127-14"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Elderkin-127">[127]</a></sup> Barred owls have a variable reaction to human disturbance. Some parent owls remain sitting, while some leave the nest upon a person's approach and some attack people climbing to the nest, as well as those approaching young on or near the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-37"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dunstan_54-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Dunstan-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Henderson_225-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Henderson-225">[225]</a></sup> There are several accounts of barred owls engaging in fierce nest defense against humans, sometimes reportedly knocking people out of trees.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-34"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-38"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Henderson_225-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Henderson-225">[225]</a></sup> However, their aggression is variable and, based on studies of similar owls like tawny and Ural owls from Europe, attacks tend to only occur when intrusions and disturbance by humans are frequent. A distraction display was recorded by a female barred owl when humans approached. In it, she spread and quivered her wings and engaged in chittering and squealing, somewhat similar to the sounds made by begging young.<sup id="cite_ref-226"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Barred-owl-chick-58.jpg)

A chick nuzzles its mother after leaving its nest.

The average hatchling weighs about 46 g (1.6 oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-39"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Elderkin_127-15"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Elderkin-127">[127]</a></sup> Like most birds, the young are initially [altricial](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Altriciality "Altriciality").<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-39"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup> The white [down](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Down_(feather) "Down (feather)") feathers that the barred owls are hatched with is replaced by white-tipped barred-buff second down at two to three weeks of age, correspondingly with growth of the wing primaries.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-40"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup> The earlier chicks are bigger and stronger, while the latter ones may not survive if food is scarce.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_26-16"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hume-26">[26]</a></sup> Size differences between barred owl nestlings may be less pronounced if the female does not begin incubation until the laying of the second egg.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-41"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup> The larger young may fight with the younger siblings for food.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_26-17"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hume-26">[26]</a></sup> The young barred owls first start moving about the nest at around this 3 week point and may start to perform threat displays if scared.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-42"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Varchmin_227-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Varchmin-227">[227]</a></sup> Adult-like feathers begin to appear at six weeks of age, starting at the scapulars, then radiating down across the abdomen and flanks up through the upper breast, with the last wisps of down remaining for up to 4 months.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-43"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_42-40"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Bent-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Varchmin_227-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Varchmin-227">[227]</a></sup> The young often start exploring around the nest tree, often falling to ground, which makes them quite vulnerable to predation despite the parents' continued protection. However, the fledglings can usually clamber back up the tree using their feet and bills, constantly wing-flapping.<sup id="cite_ref-Elderkin_127-16"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Elderkin-127">[127]</a></sup>

Fledging occurs at about 36–39 days.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-35"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup> After that, both parents continue to hunt for the young but prey deliveries taper off as the young begin to make their first flights and practice hunting.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_26-18"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hume-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dunstan_54-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Dunstan-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Varchmin_227-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Varchmin-227">[227]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-228"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup> Short flights are typical when the young barred owls are up to around 10 weeks old with longer flights commencing at about 14 weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-Varchmin_227-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Varchmin-227">[227]</a></sup> By the time they are flying and hunting somewhat assuredly in early to mid-autumn, the young move away and gain independence.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_26-19"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hume-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_65-9"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Johnsgard-65">[65]</a></sup> Dispersal of 5 young from Nova Scotia was 0.8 to 64 km (0.50 to 39.77 mi) away from their nests of origin. Furthermore, a recovered 6 month old was 8 km (5.0 mi) away from its nest of origin, while five encountered as adults were found at a distance of 16 and 43 km (9.9 and 26.7 mi) from the nests where they hatched.<sup id="cite_ref-Elderkin_127-17"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Elderkin-127">[127]</a></sup> An exceptional dispersal distance from Nova Scotia was 1,600 km (990 mi) away to [Stoney Creek, Ontario](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stoney_Creek,_Ontario "Stoney Creek, Ontario").<sup id="cite_ref-Elderkin_127-18"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Elderkin-127">[127]</a></sup> The post-dispersal stage is one of great mortality among young owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_26-20"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hume-26">[26]</a></sup> Usually juvenile owls are considered "floaters", often hovering around the perimeter of fiercely defended territories for some time. In some areas, floaters may make use of nest boxes more readily than mature birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Nicholls_82-7"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Nicholls-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Elderkin_127-19"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Elderkin-127">[127]</a></sup> Yearlings have been known to breed only very rarely, otherwise barred owl usually first breed when they are around two (sometimes three) years old. Therefore, around two years old is considered the age of maturity.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-44"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hybridization_229-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hybridization-229">[229]</a></sup> The percentages of successful nests (i.e. at least 1 fledgling produced per attempt), per study were found to be: 86% of 22 clutches in Minnesota (with a mean of 2.42 fledglings per successful attempt),<sup id="cite_ref-Johnson2_221-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Johnson2-221">[221]</a></sup> 66% of 114 clutches in Michigan (with a mean of 1.97 fledglings per successful attempt),<sup id="cite_ref-Postupalsky_190-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Postupalsky-190">[190]</a></sup> 25% of 6 clutches in Maryland (with a mean of 1 fledgling per all attempts and 1.48 per successful attempts),<sup id="cite_ref-Devereux_151-5"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Devereux-151">[151]</a></sup> 69% of 48 clutches in Nova Scotia (with a mean of 0.25 fledgling per all attempts and 2 per successful attempts)<sup id="cite_ref-Elderkin_127-20"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Elderkin-127">[127]</a></sup> and 50% of 12 nests in Washington state. Little is known about the specific factors that dictate breeding success, but are likely to include the quality of the nesting site, the food supply in the area, the levels of disturbance from outside actors (usually humans) and the maturity of the pair.

## Longevity and parasitism [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Barred_owl&action=edit&section=15 "Edit section: Longevity and parasitism")

The barred owl is a naturally long-living bird. Some record lifespans recorded per banding studies in the wild are 18 years 2 months (initial banding [Kentucky](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kentucky "Kentucky"), died in [Ohio](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ohio "Ohio")) and 24 years and 1 month in Minnesota.<sup id="cite_ref-230"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Orfinger_231-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Orfinger-231">[231]</a></sup> There are several records of breeding barred owls nesting successfully for a decade or more.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-45"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_65-10"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Johnsgard-65">[65]</a></sup> The record lifespan for a barred owl in captivity, where many animals can live longer without the stresses of surviving in wild conditions, was 34 years and 1 month, with six records of captive barred owls living over 30 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Orfinger_231-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Orfinger-231">[231]</a></sup> Known causes of mortality are diverse, some due to predation (largely great horned owls, for nestlings to adults, and probably raccoons, for eggs, nestlings and fledglings).<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-46"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_65-11"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Johnsgard-65">[65]</a></sup> Some mortality is known to occur during hunting accidents.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-47"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup> There are cases of barred owls being inadvertently killed in [furbearer traps](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trapping "Trapping").<sup id="cite_ref-232"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> Sometimes deaths occurs due to the defenses of prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_9-36"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Voous-9">[9]</a></sup> One barred owl was killed by the bite of an unknown rodent that was likely defending itself.<sup id="cite_ref-233"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup> Some cases of [cannibalism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cannibalism "Cannibalism") between adult barred owls have been reported as well.<sup id="cite_ref-234"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup> This species, like other forest owls, is vulnerable to the respiratory disease [aspergillosis](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aspergillosis "Aspergillosis").<sup id="cite_ref-235"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup> Spontaneous infections of [West Nile fever](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Nile_fever "West Nile fever") are also known to kill wild barred owls.<sup id="cite_ref-236"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> Barred owls were the most regularly infected with West Nile Virus of owl species in Georgia, as about 15% of studied barred tested positive (still at a lower rate than some hawks).<sup id="cite_ref-237"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup> Cases of [Avipoxvirus](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Avipoxvirus "Avipoxvirus") are also known in barred owls but, as with West Nile virus, the rate of mortality to viral infection is not well-known.<sup id="cite_ref-238"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup> A case of [follicular thyroid cancer](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Follicular_thyroid_cancer "Follicular thyroid cancer") was found in a wild barred owl that was unable to continue to fly.<sup id="cite_ref-239"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup> [Fatty liver diseases](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fatty_liver_disease "Fatty liver disease") have been recorded for barred owls in captivity.<sup id="cite_ref-240"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> [Eye lesions](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eye_injury "Eye injury") are fairly common in barred owls, but can be survived and possibly [surgically](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surgery "Surgery") corrected.<sup id="cite_ref-241"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Barred_Owl_with_Fishing_Line_(8158631483).jpg)

A barred owl that drowned after entanglement with fishing line.

[Parasitism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parasitism "Parasitism") is frequent in barred owls as expected for many different kinds of wild birds.<sup id="cite_ref-242"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup> The parasite load of [helminths](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helminth "Helminth") was determined to be higher in larger owls in Florida like the barred and great horned owl and lower in smaller owl species, like [eastern screech owl](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_screech_owl "Eastern screech owl").<sup id="cite_ref-243"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> While parasitic infestations are not typically detected as direct mortality causes, in some severe cases complications or direct death can occur. These cases of mortality are most widely recorded in cases of [toxoplasmosis](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toxoplasmosis "Toxoplasmosis"), although generally owls have lower rates of dangerous _[Toxoplasma](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toxoplasma "Toxoplasma")_ parasites than some other species of birds of prey.<sup id="cite_ref-244"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-245"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup> Of barred owls in one study, 26.7% had _[Toxoplasma gondii](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toxoplasma_gondii "Toxoplasma gondii")_, the cause of toxoplasmosis, upon necropsy study, with adults having it more often than immature birds.<sup id="cite_ref-246"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup> Lethal cases of [trichomoniasis](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trichomoniasis "Trichomoniasis") are also known.<sup id="cite_ref-247"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup> The effect of other parasites are more poorly known beyond generally compromising their hosts' condition, but in one case _[Sarcocystis strixi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Sarcocystis_strixi&action=edit&redlink=1 "Sarcocystis strixi (page does not exist)")_ may have caused [paralysis](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paralysis "Paralysis") in a barred owl.<sup id="cite_ref-248"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup> Other parasites recorded in barred owls include _[Eimeria](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eimeria "Eimeria") varia_,<sup id="cite_ref-249"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup> _[Novyella](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Novyella "Novyella")_,<sup id="cite_ref-250"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup> _[Neodiplostomum](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diplostomidae "Diplostomidae")_<sup id="cite_ref-251"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup> as well as apparently rare species of _[Centrorhynchus](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centrorhynchidae "Centrorhynchidae")_ and _[Tetrameres](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tetrameridae "Tetrameridae")_ that were first found within barred owls.<sup id="cite_ref-252"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-253"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup> [Trematode flatworms](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trematoda "Trematoda") such as [strigeids](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strigeidae "Strigeidae") as well as _[Tylodelphys](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tylodelphys "Tylodelphys")_ and _[Brachylaima](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brachylaima "Brachylaima")_ are found in intermediate volume in barred owls relative to other owls.<sup id="cite_ref-254"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-255"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-256"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup> Invertebrates such as [hippoboscid flies](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hippoboscidae "Hippoboscidae") and _[Mallophaga](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mallophaga "Mallophaga")_ are known to infect barred owls as [ectoparasites](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parasitism "Parasitism") but seldom at severe levels.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-48"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-257"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup> Concern was expressed that barred owls may compromise spotted owl populations with novel parasites.<sup id="cite_ref-258"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup> However, studies of _[Haemoproteus](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haemoproteus "Haemoproteus")_ in barred and spotted owls in northwestern California found that native parasites were finding a new host in barred owls and may have been transmitted from spotted to barred owls but that the barred owl acted as a poor parasite host and may have diluted the parasite levels in owl populations. Furthermore, there was no evidence found that barred owls were spreading parasites to spotted owls.<sup id="cite_ref-259"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup>

## Status [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Barred_owl&action=edit&section=16 "Edit section: Status")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Barred_Owl_Skeleton.jpg)

Barred owl skeleton ([Museum of Osteology](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Museum_of_Osteology "Museum of Osteology"))

The barred owl is one of the most common owls in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_26-21"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hume-26">[26]</a></sup> [Partners in Flight](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Partners_in_Flight "Partners in Flight") estimates that the barred owl may number up to 3 million individuals globally, making it, perhaps, the second most numerous North American owl behind the [great horned owl](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_horned_owl "Great horned owl") and perhaps slightly ahead of other commoner species like [barn owls](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barn_owl "Barn owl") and [northern saw-whet owls](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_saw-whet_owl "Northern saw-whet owl").<sup id="cite_ref-AllAbout_260-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-AllAbout-260">[260]</a></sup> It estimated that Canada in total holds about 10,000–50,000 pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-261"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-261">[261]</a></sup> [Canadian Maritimes](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Maritimes "The Maritimes") had highest densities known, with 3600 pairs, being found in 80% of 10 km<sup>2</sup> (3.9 sq mi) blocks totaling 377; this was a conspicuously higher density than that recorded in Ontario, where they were in 28% of 1824 study blocks.<sup id="cite_ref-Sauer_262-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Sauer-262">[262]</a></sup> Iowa, Tennessee and Maryland had the highest densities of pairs apparently in the United States, being present from 39 to 47% of blocks.<sup id="cite_ref-Sauer_262-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Sauer-262">[262]</a></sup> Throughout the year, the mean number of sightings per route is 0.12 sightings while during [Christmas Bird Counts](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christmas_Bird_Count "Christmas Bird Count") (CBC), the mean in 0.25 per route.<sup id="cite_ref-Sauer_262-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Sauer-262">[262]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-263"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-263">[263]</a></sup>

## Barred owl and northern spotted owl [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Barred_owl&action=edit&section=17 "Edit section: Barred owl and northern spotted owl")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Barred_owl.jpg)

[Gatineau Park](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gatineau_Park "Gatineau Park"), [Gatineau](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gatineau "Gatineau"), [Quebec](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quebec "Quebec")

Barred owls are thought to be partly responsible for the recent decline of the [northern spotted owl](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_spotted_owl "Northern spotted owl"), native to British Columbia, [Washington](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Washington_(state) "Washington (state)"), [Oregon](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oregon "Oregon"), and [California](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/California "California").<sup id="cite_ref-264"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-265"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup> The 2011 Revised Recovery Plan for the Northern Spotted Owl states "Based on the best available scientific information, competition from the barred owl (_S. varia_) poses a significant and complex threat to the spotted owl."<sup id="cite_ref-266"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup> Northern spotted owls were thought to be already declining considerably before barred owls moved into their range, mostly in sync with large-scale logging operations and land development carried out by humans, with their problems now further exacerbated by the barred species's presence.<sup id="cite_ref-Buchanan_10-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Buchanan-10">[10]</a></sup> There are ecological discrepancies between the species in areas of sympatry. While both species prefer old-growth forest areas, the spotted owls tend to live in significantly higher elevation areas in Washington's Cascade mountains, with nest sites averaging 966.2 m (3,170 ft) [meters above sea level](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meters_above_sea_level "Meters above sea level") against a mean of 54.1 m (177 ft) for barred owls, in areas with more steep slopes.<sup id="cite_ref-267"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-268"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-268">[268]</a></sup> The barred owls of the area additionally prefer wetter, more variable forest areas, down to [riparian zones](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Riparian_zone "Riparian zone") with limited forested land, while the spotted tended to cluster in more homogeneous upland and ancient [coniferous forests](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pinophyta "Pinophyta").<sup id="cite_ref-Herter_269-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Herter-269">[269]</a></sup> However, little is known on the feasibility or benefits of habitat maintenance that favors spotted over barred owls. Habitat management measures are hypothesized to be insufficient to mitigate the negative effect the barred owls are having.<sup id="cite_ref-270"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-270">[270]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-271"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-271">[271]</a></sup> In areas where barred owls moved in within 0.8 km (0.50 mi) of a spotted owl nest area, 39% of spotted owls disappeared and were not seen again, while in areas still free of barred owls (so spared from this interspecific pressure), 11% of spotted owls disappeared and were not found again.<sup id="cite_ref-Kelly_6-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Kelly-6">[6]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Barred_Owl_(Audubon).jpg)

[Audubon](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_James_Audubon "John James Audubon")'s illustration of a barred owl attempting to attack a [squirrel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Squirrel "Squirrel").

Since the 1960s, barred owls have been expanding their range westward from the eastern US and Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-272"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-272">[272]</a></sup> While some authors have described the expansion of barred owls into the west as "natural",<sup id="cite_ref-273"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-273">[273]</a></sup> others state that this is a mischaracterization, as there is little to suggest that the barred owls could have reached the spotted's range without the inadvertent aid of humans — increasing temperatures, wildlife suppression, and tree planting likely facilitated the range increase.<sup id="cite_ref-274"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-274">[274]</a></sup> When spotted owls and barred owls share the same area, the barred owls generally are more aggressive and out-compete the spotted owls, leading to decreased populations of the native owls.<sup id="cite_ref-275"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-275">[275]</a></sup> The more aggressive response of barred owls to interspecific stimuli has been verified with experiments incorporating vocalization and owl dummies.<sup id="cite_ref-276"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-276">[276]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-277"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-277">[277]</a></sup> Additionally, the adult survival rates appear higher for barred owls (an estimated 91%) than for spotted owls (an estimated 82%). Therefore, the barred owl is considered "demographically superior".<sup id="cite_ref-Wiens_123-21"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Wiens-123">[123]</a></sup> In December 2023, the [U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/U.S._Fish_and_Wildlife_Service "U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service") issued a draft of a management plan indicating that hunters in the Pacific Northwest would be encouraged to shoot more than a half million barred owls. The rationale was to cull the numbers of this invasive species, to help protect native species, particularly the spotted owl which had been listed as threatened under the Endangered Species Act.<sup id="cite_ref-278"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-278">[278]</a></sup>

Another potential threat is that barred owls and spotted owls occasionally interbreed, creating hybrids ("sparred owl" or "botted owl").<sup id="cite_ref-Hybridization_229-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hybridization-229">[229]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-279"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-279">[279]</a></sup> Only 47 hybrids with barred owls (all between female barred owls and male spotted owls) were found in an analysis of more than 9,000 banded spotted owls; consequently, hybridization between these two species is considered to be "an interesting biological phenomenon that is probably inconsequential compared with the real threat—direct competition between the two species for food and space".<sup id="cite_ref-Hybridization_229-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hybridization-229">[229]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kelly&amp;Forsman2004_280-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Kelly&amp;Forsman2004-280">[280]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-281"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-281">[281]</a></sup> Most hybrids favor their barred owl heritage with similar and more pale overall coloring than spotted owls, though the back tends to be intermediate in color between the two and the beak coloring is variable. However, there has been much variation described.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_7-49"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-BNA-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hybridization_229-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Hybridization-229">[229]</a></sup> Due to variances in appearance, previously hybrids between the species were overestimated from field identification, only being certainly identified by genetic [loci](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Locus_(genetics) "Locus (genetics)").<sup id="cite_ref-282"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-282">[282]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-283"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-283">[283]</a></sup> Moreover, no recent hybrids could be detected in a survey of the [Cascade Mountains](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cascade_Mountains "Cascade Mountains").<sup id="cite_ref-Herter_269-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Herter-269">[269]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-284"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-284">[284]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-285"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-285">[285]</a></sup>

## In art [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Barred_owl&action=edit&section=18 "Edit section: In art")

[John James Audubon](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_James_Audubon "John James Audubon") illustrated the barred owl in _[Birds of America](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Birds_of_America_(book) "Birds of America (book)")_ (published in London, 1827–1838) as Plate 46, where it is shown threatening a [grey squirrel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_gray_squirrel "Eastern gray squirrel"). The image was engraved and colored by [Robert Havell](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Havell "Robert Havell")'s London workshops. The original [aquatint](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aquatint "Aquatint") by Audubon is owned by the Brooklyn Museum.<sup id="cite_ref-Brooklyn_Museum_286-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-Brooklyn_Museum-286">[286]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-287"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barred_owl#cite_note-287">[287]</a></sup>
